reference PyTorch source (the prompt the agent sees)
import torch
import torch.nn as nn

class Model(nn.Module):
    """
    Model that performs a transposed convolution, multiplies by a scalar, applies global average pooling, 
    another global average pooling
    """
    def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, output_padding, multiplier):
        super(Model, self).__init__()
        self.conv_transpose = nn.ConvTranspose2d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding, output_padding=output_padding)
        self.multiplier = multiplier

    def forward(self, x):
        x = self.conv_transpose(x)
        x = x * self.multiplier
        x = torch.mean(x, dim=[2, 3], keepdim=True)  # First global average pooling
        x = torch.mean(x, dim=[2, 3], keepdim=True)  # Second global average pooling
        return x

batch_size = 16
in_channels = 64
out_channels = 128
height, width = 128, 128
kernel_size = 3
stride = 2
padding = 1
output_padding = 1
multiplier = 0.5

def get_inputs():
    return [torch.rand(batch_size, in_channels, height, width)]

def get_init_inputs():
    return [in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, output_padding, multiplier]

Let's compute: (128-1)*2 = 254. -2*1 = -2 => 252. +kernel_size=3 =>255. +output_padding=1 =>256. Yes H_out=W_out=256. Then multiply by 0.5. Then mean over dims [2,3] keepdim=True: shape becomes [batch_size, out_channels, 1, 1]. Then another mean over [2,3] keepdim=True: since the last two dims are 1, mean of a single element is that element, so shape stays [batch_size, out_channels, 1, 1]. So effectively the output is just the average of each channel over the 256x256 spatial grid of the conv transpose output times multiplier. So the entire forward is: for each batch and each output channel, compute sum_{h,w} conv_transpose_output[b,c_out,h,w] * multiplier / (H_out*W_out). Since mean over dims [2,3] twice: first mean divides by H_out*W_out, second mean divides by 1*1 (so no effect). Actually first mean: x.mean(dim=[2,3], keepdim=True) = sum / (H*W). Then second mean: x.mean(dim=[2,3], keepdim=True) = sum / (1*1) = same. So overall each output is (sum of conv_transpose output * multiplier) / (H_out*W_out). But maybe there is a mathematical simplification: The conv_transpose is a linear operation: output = W * x + b (where W is the weight matrix, x is input). Then global average pooling of output is equivalent to applying global average pooling to each output feature map. Actually transposed convolution can be expressed as conv2d on a dilated input. But maybe we can compute the sum over output space by summing the kernels. Summing over all output spatial locations yields each output channel sum = sum_{i,j} (weight * input) over all positions. Since convolution is linear and shift-invariant (except boundaries), the sum over all output positions is equivalent to convolving the input with the same kernel but summing over output. This is equivalent to computing the correlation of the input with the "sum of the kernel over spatial positions". In the direct convolution view: we have a dilated input x_dilated of shape (N, C_in, (H_in-1)*stride + 1, (W_in-1)*stride + 1). What is w_conv? It should be w_tr permuted: from [C_in, C_out, K, K] to [C_out, C_in, K, K]. Let's verify. Take a 1D example. Input x [2], weight w_tr [1,1,2] (in=1, out=1, K=2). stride=1, padding=0. ConvTranspose1d output should be length 3 (2+2-1). The operation is cross-correlation: output[0] = x[0]*w[0]; output[1] = x[0]*w[1] + x[1]*w[0]; output[2] = x[1]*w[1]. Now standard conv1d on dilated input (no zeros inserted since stride=1): x_padded = x padded with K-1-p = 2-1-0 = 1 zero on each side: [0, x0, x1, 0]. Then conv with weight w_conv of shape [out=1, in=1, K=2]. If w_conv = w_tr? Let's try w_conv = [w0, w1]. Conv on padded input: output[0] = 0*w0 + x0*w1 = x0*w1. output[1] = x0*w0 + x1*w1. output[2] = x1*w0 + 0*w1 = x1*w0. That's reversed: w0 and w1 are swapped relative to the transposed conv formula. So to match, we need to either flip the kernel or something. In 2D, conv2d performs cross-correlation (no flip). The transposed conv is equivalent to conv2d on dilated input padded with (K - 1 - padding) using the same weight but with channels swapped and spatially flipped? Actually it's equivalent to conv2d with weight rotated 180 degrees (i.e., flipped both spatial dims) and channel dims swapped? Let's verify. In PyTorch, ConvTranspose2d uses the weight as given (no flipping). The equivalent direct convolution uses the same weight but with channel dims swapped? Let's check docs. From PyTorch docs for ConvTranspose2d: "The transposed convolution operator multiplies each input value element-wise by a learnable kernel, and sums over the outputs from all input feature planes." It also says: "This module can be seen as the gradient of Conv2d with respect to its input." And: "It is also known as a fractionally-strided convolution or a deconvolution (although it is not an actual deconvolution operation)." The shape of weight is (in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size[0], kernel_size[1]). If we want to compute the output as a direct convolution on dilated input, the weight should be of shape (out_channels, in_channels, K, K). So we need to permute the first two dims of w_tr. Additionally, because conv2d performs cross-correlation (which is convolution with flipped kernel), and conv_transpose is the transpose of that, we might need to spatially flip the kernel. Let's reason. Consider conv2d with weight w of shape (C_out, C_in, K, K). It computes y = w ⋆ x (cross-correlation). The linear operator is A. The transpose A^T corresponds to conv_transpose2d with weight w_tr = w.permute(1,0,2,3) (swap channel dims) but with spatial flipping? Let's check in 1D. Conv1d with weight w = [w0, w1] (cross-correlation). Input x = [x0, x1, x2]. Output y = [y0, y1] with stride=1, padding=0, K=2. y0 = x0*w0 + x1*w1. y1 = x1*w0 + x2*w1. Matrix A (y = A x): A = [[w0, w1, 0], [0, w0, w1]] Now conv_transpose1d with weight w_tr = what? The transpose A^T: A^T = [[w0, 0], [w1, w0], [0, w1]] So output z = A^T y: z0 = w0*y0 z1 = w1*y0 + w0*y1 z2 = w1*y1 Now, can we express this as conv1d on dilated input? Input to conv_transpose is y (length 2). Dilate with stride=1: y_dilated = y (length 2). Pad with (K-1 - padding) = (2-1-0) = 1 zero on each side: [0, y0, y1, 0]. Then conv1d with some weight w_conv of length 2: If w_conv = [w0, w1]: output0 = 0*w0 + y0*w1 = y0*w1 output1 = y0*w0 + y1*w1 output2 = y1*w0 + 0*w1 = y1*w0 This gives [y0*w1, y0*w0 + y1*w1, y1*w0] which is not [w0*y0, w1*y0 + w0*y1, w1*y1]. The weights are swapped (w0 and w1 reversed) in the first and last terms? Actually output0 = y0*w1 = w1*y0; but we need w0*y0. So w_conv needs to be [w1, w0] (spatially reversed). Let's check w_conv = [w1, w0]: output0 = 0*w1 + y0*w0 = y0*w0. Good. output1 = y0*w1 + y1*w0 = w1*y0 + w0*y1. Good. output2 = y1*w1 + 0*w0 = w1*y1. Good. So spatial flipping is needed. In 2D, we need to flip both height and width dimensions of the kernel. Then permute channel dims: from w_tr (in, out, H, W) to w_conv (out, in, H, W) = w_tr.permute(1,0,2,3).flip(2,3)? Let's verify. The conv2d weight is w (out, in, H, W). The transpose uses w_tr = w.permute(1,0,2,3). Actually w_tr is the weight parameter of ConvTranspose2d. So w = w_tr.permute(1,0,2,3). To express conv_transpose as a conv on dilated input, we need to use w_conv = w.flip(2,3) = w_tr.permute(1,0,2,3).flip(2,3). But maybe it's easier to just use the "scatter" view of conv_transpose2d to compute the sum. Alternatively, we can compute the sum by using the fact that the sum of the output of a transposed convolution is equal to the sum over input pixels times the sum of the kernel weights, but with appropriate scaling due to stride and padding? Let's think carefully about the sum of all output pixels. In the matrix view, output vector y (size N*C_out*H_out*W_out) = A^T * x_vec, where x_vec is the input (size N*C_in*H_in*W_in) and A is the conv2d matrix with weight w (out, in, K, K). Note: w here is the weight that would correspond to a conv2d. But our ConvTranspose2d weight is w_tr = w.permute(1,0,2,3). But we don't need to think about that; we can think directly about the linear operator defined by ConvTranspose2d. Actually, maybe there is an even simpler approach: Since we need the global average of the output, which is a scalar per channel per batch, we can compute it by performing a standard convolution of the input with the sum-aggregated kernel. Let's define the operation explicitly. Let input x shape: (N, C_in, H_in, W_in). Weight w shape: (C_in, C_out, K, K). Transposed conv output y shape: (N, C_out, H_out, W_out). The formula for y[n, c_out, h, w] is: y[n, c_out, h, w] = sum_{c_in=0}^{C_in-1} sum_{k_h=0}^{K-1} sum_{k_w=0}^{K-1} x[n, c_in, h', w'] * w[c_in, c_out, k_h, k_w] where h' and w' are the input coordinates that map to output (h,w) with kernel offset (k_h,k_w). What is the exact relationship? From the "dilated input then conv" view: Let x_dilated be x with zeros inserted: x_dilated[n, c_in, h_d, w_d] = x[n, c_in, h, w] if h_d = h*stride and w_d = w*stride; else 0. Actually for stride=s, the dilated input has size (H_in-1)*s + 1. But since we pad with p' = K - 1 - padding on each side, the indices align differently. Better: Use the standard definition from PyTorch for ConvTranspose2d. Let's derive the mapping from output (h,w) to input (h',w'). In the equivalent conv2d view: conv_transpose2d is the same as conv2d on dilated input with effective kernel = w_flipped (both dims), stride=1, padding = K - 1 - padding. The input is dilated by stride. Let's test with 1D example: stride=2, padding=1, K=3, output_padding=1, H_in=128. Dilated input size: (128-1)*2+1=255. Pad with K-1-padding = 3-1-1 = 1 on each side => 257. Conv with K=3, stride=1, pad=0 => output 255. Output padding adds 1 => 256. That matches. So the mapping for output index h_out (0-based) to dilated input index i is: i = h_out (since conv stride=1, pad=0). The dilated input index i corresponds to original input index h_in = floor(i / stride) if i % stride == 0, else it's a zero (since dilated). But because of padding p' = K-1-padding added on the left, the actual input coordinate is i - p'. So h_out maps to i = h_out. The original input coordinate h_in = (h_out - p') / stride if (h_out - p') % stride == 0, else it's a zero (from dilation). And the kernel offset for the conv is k = i - (h_out) ... wait, in a standard conv with pad=0, output h_out corresponds to input window starting at h_out, covering indices h_out, h_out+1, ..., h_out+K-1. But we padded the dilated input, so the effective window covers indices from h_out to h_out+K-1 in the padded dilated input. The padded dilated input indices from 0 to p'-1 are zeros (left padding). So for a given output h_out, the original input pixel that contributes with kernel offset k is at padded index i = h_out + k. The original coordinate (before padding) is i - p'. This corresponds to input index h_in = (i - p') / stride if (i - p') % stride == 0 and i - p' >= 0 and h_in < H_in. Otherwise it's zero. Alternatively, think of the transposed conv as each input pixel x[n,c_in,h,w] being multiplied by the kernel and added to output patch with top-left corner at (h*stride - padding, w*stride - padding). Wait, is that correct? Let's test with the 1D example: stride=2, padding=1, K=3. Input x0 at h=0: top-left = 0*2 - 1 = -1. So patch covers output indices -1,0,1. Valid: 0,1. That's consistent with earlier scatter view. Input x1 at h=1: top-left = 1*2 - 1 = 1. Patch covers 1,2,3. So output indices 1,2,3. This matches the convolution on dilated padded input? Let's see. This is not the same. Wait, my padded conv view gave x0 in window for output 0 only, and x1 in output 0,1,2? Let's recompute. Let's take a concrete example: H_in=2, stride=2, padding=1, K=3, output_padding=1. x = [x0, x1]. Dilated x_d = [x0, 0, x1]. Pad left with p' = K-1-padding = 3-1-1 = 1, right with 1 + output_padding? Actually in conv2d transpose, output_padding adds extra zeros to the right/bottom of the dilated input? Or to the output? It adds extra rows/cols to the output. In the direct conv view, output_padding just increases the output size, which corresponds to adding extra padding to the right/bottom of the input? Let's see: H_out = H_in' + 2p' - K + 1 + output_padding. So output_padding effectively adds output_padding to the right side (or bottom). So padding is asymmetric: left/top = K-1-padding, right/bottom = K-1-padding + output_padding. Let's test with numbers: H_in'=3, p'=1, output_padding=1. L_in = 3 + 2*1 + 1 = 6. Wait, we established earlier that to match transpose conv, the direct conv needs to use the spatially flipped kernel. Let's assume weight for direct conv is w_flip = w_tr permuted and flipped. But let's step back. The key is: can we compute the sum of y efficiently without computing y? The sum S[n, c_out] = sum_{h,w} y[n, c_out, h, w]. Let's write the explicit summation using the scatter view. Scatter view: For each input pixel x[n, c_in, h, w] and each kernel offset (k_h, k_w), we add x * w[c_in, c_out, k_h, k_w] to output pixel at (h*stride - padding + k_h, w*stride - padding + k_w). However, output pixels outside [0, H_out-1] x [0, W_out-1] are discarded. Thus the total sum S[n, c_out] = sum_{c_in, h, w, k_h, k_w} x[n, c_in, h, w] * w[c_in, c_out, k_h, k_w] * I_valid(h*stride - padding + k_h, w*stride - padding + k_w), where I_valid is 1 if the coordinate is inside the output bounds, else 0. Since x and w are independent of the validity condition, we can factor: S[n, c_out] = sum_{c_in} ( sum_{h,w} x[n, c_in, h, w] * A_{c_in, c_out}(h,w) ), where A_{c_in, c_out}(h,w) = sum_{k_h,k_w} w[c_in, c_out, k_h, k_w] * I_valid(h*stride - padding + k_h, ...). But A depends on (h,w) because for input pixels near the border, some kernel offsets map outside the output and are discarded. So it's not simply a constant factor across all spatial locations. However, maybe due to the specific sizes, the output is large enough that all input pixels produce full KxK patches inside the output? Let's check. For our configuration: H_in = 128, stride=2, padding=1, output_padding=1, K=3, H_out=256. Input pixel at (h,w) produces patch top-left at (2h - 1, 2w - 1). The patch covers rows 2h-1 to 2h+1, cols 2w-1 to 2w+1. Are these always within [0,255]? Let's check extremes: h=0: rows -1,0,1. Row -1 is out of bounds, so only rows 0,1 are valid. So top rows are cropped. h=127: rows 253,254,255. All valid. Bottom rows are fine. Similarly w=0: cols -1,0,1. Col -1 out. w=127: cols 253,254,255. All valid. So the first row and first column of input pixels have partial contributions (cropped by 1 row/col). All other input pixels have full 3x3 patches inside the output. Since the image is large (128x128), the boundary effect is small. But for exactness, we need to account for it. However, maybe we can avoid dealing with the boundaries by using the "valid" region and adding corrections. Or perhaps we can compute the sum using a convolution: the sum over all output spatial locations of a transposed convolution is equal to the sum over input spatial locations of a convolution with the same kernel but padded appropriately. Actually there is a known identity: the global sum of the output of a transposed convolution is equal to the global sum of the input times the global sum of the kernel weights, provided that the transposed convolution does not crop any contributions? Let B be matrix with rows corresponding to output of B (z-space) and columns corresponding to input of B (z'-space, i.e., y-space). So B has shape (dim_z, dim_y). Then B^T has shape (dim_y, dim_z). Our ConvTranspose2d computes y_vec = B^T * z_vec. So (B^T)_{j,i} = B_{i,j}. Here j indexes y-space (output of transpose), i indexes z-space (input to transpose). Now sum_j y_j = sum_j sum_i (B^T)_{j,i} z_i = sum_i z_i sum_j (B^T)_{j,i} = sum_i z_i sum_j B_{i,j}. But sum_j B_{i,j} is the sum of row i of B. Since rows of B correspond to output pixels of B (z-space), and columns correspond to input pixels of B (y-space). So sum_j B_{i,j} is the sum of the i-th row of B. The i-th row of B corresponds to a particular output pixel of the conv2d B, and its entries are the kernel weights that connect input pixels to that output pixel. So sum_j B_{i,j} is simply the sum of all kernel weights for that output pixel, regardless of input pixel, because each input pixel in the receptive field contributes w_B[c_in, c_out, k_h, k_w] and there are no other terms. But wait, the row has entries w_B[c_in, c_out, k_h, k_w] for each column j that falls in the receptive field. So sum_j B_{i,j} = sum_{c_out, k_h, k_w} w_B[c_in, c_out, k_h, k_w] where c_in is fixed by the row i's channel. Actually row i = (n, c_in, h_z, w_z). The non-zero columns j are (n, c_out, h_y, w_y) where h_y = h_z*stride - padding + k_h? No, for conv2d B (stride=2, padding=1), the input pixel (c_out, h_y, w_y) contributes to output (c_in, h_z, w_z) if h_y is in the receptive field of (h_z,w_z). The standard conv2d mapping: for output (h_z,w_z), the top-left of the input window is (h_z*stride - padding, w_z*stride - padding) = (2*h_z - 1, 2*w_z - 1). The input pixel at (h_y,w_y) contributes with kernel offset (k_h = h_y - (2*h_z - 1), k_w = w_y - (2*w_z - 1)). So for a given output pixel, the receptive field is the set of input pixels (h_y, w_y) such that k_h,k_w in [0,K-1]. There are K*K such input pixels per channel c_out. Thus row i of B has exactly K*K non-zero entries for each c_out, each equal to w_B[c_in, c_out, k_h, k_w]. Therefore sum_j B_{i,j} = sum_{c_out=0}^{C_out-1} sum_{k_h,k_w} w_B[c_in, c_out, k_h, k_w]. Importantly, this sum does NOT depend on spatial location (h_z,w_z) at all! It only depends on c_in (the output channel of B, which is the input channel of our transpose). Wait, could there be cropping? For h_z=0, the top-left is -1. The receptive field includes h_y = -1,0,1. But h_y=-1 is out of bounds, so those entries are missing. Actually in conv2d, if padding=1, the input is conceptually padded with 1 zero on each side. So the receptive field includes the padded zeros. The matrix B includes those padded zeros as columns? Typically, the conv matrix includes the padding implicitly: the input to conv is padded, so the linear operator includes the padding in its definition. If we consider the conv operator on the unpadded input, the matrix columns correspond only to actual input pixels (h_y in [0, H_y-1]). The padded zeros are not columns. So for output h_z=0, the receptive field includes h_y = -1 (padding zero) which is not a column. Thus the row i has fewer non-zero entries. Specifically, for h_z=0, only kernel offsets k_h=1,2 have corresponding h_y >= 0. k_h=0 maps to h_y=-1 which is absent. So there are only 2*3 = 6 non-zero entries per c_out instead of 9. Similarly for h_z=0 and w_z=0, only 2*2=4 entries. So the row sum depends on the spatial location due to boundary cropping! But wait, in standard conv2d with padding=1 applied to input of size H_y=256, the effective padding adds zeros around the input. The output h_z=0 uses input indices h_y = -1,0,1. Since h_y=-1 is a padded zero, it does not contribute. But the matrix B only has columns for actual input pixels. So the row sum for h_z=0, w_z arbitrary is missing some weights. For h_z=0, w_z=0: missing k_h=0 or k_w=0 entries: only k_h in {1,2}, k_w in {1,2}. So sum = sum_{c_out} sum_{k_h=1..2} sum_{k_w=1..2} w_B[c_in, c_out, k_h, k_w]. For h_z=0, w_z>0 and w_z<W_z-1: missing k_h=0 entries: sum = sum_{c_out} sum_{k_h=1..2} sum_{k_w=0..2} w_B... etc. So the row sum is not constant; it varies for the first/last rows/cols. However, H_y = 256, H_z = 128. For h_z=0, the top-left is -1. For h_z=127, top-left = 2*127 - 1 = 253. The window covers 253,254,255. All valid. So only the first row and first column of output z have missing contributions (due to top/left cropping). Actually also bottom/right? Let's check h_z=127: top-left=253, covers 253,254,255. Since H_y=256, indices 253,254,255 are valid. So no missing at bottom/right. So only the top and left edges are affected because of the formula top-left = h_z*stride - padding. With stride=2 and padding=1, top-left is odd numbers: -1,1,3,...,253. For top-left = -1, some kernel offsets map to -1 (invalid). For top-left = 1, all offsets 0,1,2 map to 1,2,3 which are valid. So indeed, only h_z=0 and w_z=0 have partial receptive fields. The rest are full. But wait, what about the output padding? output_padding=1 in conv_transpose2d. Does that affect the conv2d B? B is the conv2d that maps H_y=256 to H_z=128 with stride=2, padding=1. Output padding is not a parameter of conv2d; it's only for conv_transpose2d to increase the output size. But the transpose relationship requires that B maps from the conv_transpose output space to the conv_transpose input space. If conv_transpose output is 256, then B input is 256. B uses stride=2, padding=1, kernel=3. The output of B is floor((256 + 2*1 - 3)/2)+1 = floor(255/2)+1 = 128. So B is well-defined. The output_padding in conv_transpose means that the output is one larger than what it would be without output_padding. This corresponds to having a slightly larger input to B. In terms of the linear operator, B is defined for input size 256, output size 128. The padding zeros added by B are only on the left/top? No, conv2d padding adds zeros on all sides equally (padding=1). So B's input is padded with 1 zero on all four sides. But as we saw, because stride=2, the top-left window for h_z=0 starts at -1, which is outside the actual input but inside the padded region. However, the padded region includes h_y=-1 (top) and h_y=256 (bottom) etc. But our matrix B columns correspond to actual input pixels (0..255). The padded zeros are not columns. So for h_z=0, the window includes h_y=-1,0,1. h_y=-1 is a padded zero (not a column), h_y=0,1 are columns. For h_z=127, window includes 253,254,255. h_y=256 would be padded zero but is not included because k_h only goes up to 2, so 253+2=255. So bottom padding is not used. Similarly for left: w_z=0 uses w_y=-1,0,1; w_y=-1 is padded zero. Right padding: w_z=127 uses 253,254,255, no right pad. So the missing contributions are only for top and left edges. But this is for B. We need sum_j B_{i,j} for row i = (n, c_in, h_z, w_z). As established, sum_j B_{i,j} = sum_{c_out} sum_{k_h,k_w in valid for this row} w_B[c_in, c_out, k_h, k_w]. Now w_B is the weight of Conv2d B, which is the same as w_tr (the weight of ConvTranspose2d). Wait, B is a Conv2d with in_channels=C_out, out_channels=C_in, weight shape (C_in, C_out, K, K). That's exactly w_tr. So w_B[c_in, c_out, k_h, k_w] = w_tr[c_in, c_out, k_h, k_w]. So for most spatial locations (h_z != 0 and w_z != 0), sum_j B_{i,j} = sum_{c_out} sum_{k_h=0..2} sum_{k_w=0..2} w_tr[c_in, c_out, k_h, k_w]. For h_z=0, w_z!=0: missing k_h=0. So sum = sum_{c_out} sum_{k_h=1..2} sum_{k_w=0..2} w_tr[c_in, c_out, k_h, k_w]. For h_z!=0, w_z=0: missing k_w=0. So sum = sum_{c_out} sum_{k_h=0..2} sum_{k_w=1..2} w_tr[c_in, c_out, k_h, k_w]. For h_z=0, w_z=0: missing k_h=0 or k_w=0. Actually missing the top-left corner: k_h=0,k_w=0; k_h=0, any k_w; any k_h, k_w=0. So valid are k_h=1,2 and k_w=1,2. So sum = sum_{c_out} sum_{k_h=1..2} sum_{k_w=1..2} w_tr[c_in, c_out, k_h, k_w]. Now S_{n,c_out} = sum_{c_in, h_z, w_z} x[n, c_in, h_z, w_z] * sum_j B_{i,j}, where i=(n,c_in,h_z,w_z). Thus we can compute output per channel as: S[n, c_out] = sum_{c_in} ( sum_{h_z,w_z} x[n,c_in,h_z,w_z] * M_{c_in}(h_z,w_z) ) where M_{c_in}(h_z,w_z) is the spatially varying coefficient matrix described above. Specifically: - For h_z>=1 and w_z>=1: M = sum_{c_out} sum_{k_h,k_w} w_tr[c_in, c_out, k_h, k_w]. - For h_z=0, w_z>=1: M = sum_{c_out} sum_{k_h=1..2, k_w=0..2} w_tr[c_in, c_out, k_h, k_w]. - For h_z>=1, w_z=0: M = sum_{c_out} sum_{k_h=0..2, k_w=1..2} w_tr[c_in, c_out, k_h, k_w]. - For h_z=0, w_z=0: M = sum_{c_out} sum_{k_h=1..2, k_w=1..2} w_tr[c_in, c_out, k_h, k_w]. Then after computing S, we multiply by multiplier and divide by H_out * W_out = 256 * 256 = 65536. Wait, but this is just for the sum of the output. Is that correct? Let's verify with a tiny example in PyTorch. But before that, we need to be careful: The formula y_j = sum_i B_{i,j} z_i is for the standard linear transpose: y = B^T z. However, ConvTranspose2d in PyTorch also includes a bias term. In our reference, the ConvTranspose2d layer does not have bias explicitly set, but by default bias=True. However the constructor just uses ConvTranspose2d(...). The default is bias=True. But the reference model does not set bias=False. Wait, check the code: ```python self.conv_transpose = nn.ConvTranspose2d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding, output_padding=output_padding) ``` Default bias=True. So there is a bias. The forward does x = self.conv_transpose(x) -> x = x * self.multiplier -> x = torch.mean(...). The bias adds a constant to each output channel at every spatial location. Then the global average of the output includes that bias. So we need to account for bias: y[n,c_out,h,w] = (conv_transpose part) + b[c_out]. The sum over all spatial locations adds b[c_out] * (H_out * W_out). So the total sum S[n,c_out] = (sum of conv_transpose part) + b[c_out] * H_out * W_out. Then after multiplying by multiplier and dividing by H_out*W_out, the output is: out[n,c_out] = (S[n,c_out] * multiplier) / (H_out*W_out) = (sum_conv_part * multiplier) / (H_out*W_out) + b[c_out] * multiplier. Wait, check: mean over dims [2,3] keepdim=True of (conv_out * multiplier) gives a tensor of shape [N, C_out, 1, 1] where each entry is (sum_{h,w} (conv_out[n,c_out,h,w] * multiplier + b[c_out]*multiplier)) / (H_out*W_out). Actually the bias is inside the conv_transpose output, before multiplication. So x after conv_transpose = conv_part + b. Then multiplied by multiplier: (conv_part + b) * multiplier. Then mean: sum_{h,w} (conv_part * multiplier + b*multiplier) / (H_out*W_out). Since b*multiplier is constant across spatial dims, its average is b*multiplier. So output = (sum_{h,w} conv_part * multiplier) / (H_out * W_out) + b[c_out] * multiplier. Then the second mean over [2,3] (which are size 1) does nothing. So effectively output[n, c_out, 1, 1] = (sum_{h,w} conv_part[n,c_out,h,w] * multiplier) / (H_out*W_out) + b[c_out] * multiplier. Great. So if we can compute the sum of conv_part efficiently, we can compute the output. Is sum_{h,w} conv_part exactly equal to S[n,c_out] as derived? Let's verify. conv_part = ConvTranspose2d(x, weight=w_tr, bias=None). We need sum_{h,w} conv_part[n,c_out,h,w]. Using the matrix view: conv_part vector y = B^T x_vec. Sum over entries of y for fixed n,c_out is sum_{j in (n,c_out,*,*)} y_j = sum_j sum_i B_{i,j} x_i = sum_i x_i * (sum_j B_{i,j}). Here x_i = x[n, c_in, h_z, w_z]. sum_j B_{i,j} is the row sum of B for row i = (n, c_in, h_z, w_z). As we argued, this sum depends only on c_in,(h_z,w_z) and the boundary conditions. Now, we can compute sum_j B_{i,j} via a small kernel. Instead of trying to derive everything analytically, perhaps we can implement a CUDA kernel that computes S by applying a spatial reduction with boundary masking. But since H_z=128, W_z=128, K=3, C_in=64, C_out=128, N=16, we could even do this on CPU or with a simple CUDA kernel and it would be extremely fast because we skip the huge 256x256 output computation. The naive approach for S: For each n, c_out, we need sum_{c_in, h, w} x[n, c_in, h, w] * W_eff[c_in, c_out, h, w] where W_eff is the effective weight per input spatial location. But we can compute it by iterating over input pixels and accumulating. Alternatively, we can compute the sum by performing a convolution-like operation where we don't store the output spatially, but just accumulate into a per-channel sum. That is, for each input pixel x[n,c_in,h,w], we "scatter" its contribution to the per-channel sum for all c_out. Each input pixel contributes x * sum of weights that are actually applied from that pixel to the output. But the set of weights applied depends on (h,w) due to boundaries. For (h,w) not on the top/left edge, the sum of weights is just sum_{k_h,k_w} w_tr[c_in, c_out, k_h, k_w]. For h=0, it's missing the top row of the kernel (k_h=0). For w=0, missing left column (k_w=0). For h=0,w=0, missing top-left 2x2 block? Actually for h=0,w=0, missing k_h=0 and k_w=0. But what about the output_padding? Does output_padding affect the sum? The output_padding adds extra rows/cols to the bottom/right of the output. In the scatter view, output_padding means the output has more space, but does it change where the input pixels scatter? Typically, output_padding in conv_transpose2d just increases the output size by adding extra zeros to the bottom/right of the dilated input before convolution (or equivalently, it shifts the mapping). More concretely, in the "dilate then conv" view, outputpadding adds extra padding to the right/bottom of the dilated input (or removes cropping). In the scatter view, output_padding means some input pixels may have their output patches shifted or may map to additional output pixels. However, the standard formula for conv_transpose2d with output_padding is equivalent to conv_transpose2d without output_padding followed by padding the output on one side. Let's check PyTorch docs. From PyTorch docs: "The additional size added to one side of the output shape." Output padding is used to adjust the output shape when stride > 1. It doesn't change the values computed for the existing output pixels; it just appends extra rows/columns of effectively zero (or rather, the convolution extends to cover them). Actually, the output of conv_transpose2d with output_padding > 0 includes additional output pixels that are computed from the input. It's not just zero-padding the output. Let's see with a 1D example. ConvTranspose1d with input [x0, x1], weight [w0, w1], stride=2, padding=1, output_padding=1. Without output_padding: output size = (2-1)*2 - 2*1 + 2 + 0 = 2 - 2 + 2 = 2. Actually formula: (H_in - 1)*stride - 2*padding + kernel_size + output_padding = (2-1)*2 - 2 + 2 + 0 = 2. So output size 2. With output_padding=1: output size = 3. Let's compute manually using scatter: input x0 at h=0: center = 0*2 - 1 = -1. Patch covers -1,0. Input x1 at h=1: center = 1*2 - 1 = 1. Patch covers 1,2. So output indices covered: 0,1,2. That's 3 outputs. Without output_padding, would output be 2? Actually with padding=1, K=2, stride=2, the formula gives H_out = (H_in-1)*2 - 2 + 2 = 2. But scatter gives indices up to 2. Something is off. Let's recalc: For K=2, stride=2, padding=1. Input h=0: top-left = 0*2 - 1 = -1. Patch covers -1,0. h=1: top-left = 1*2 - 1 = 1. Patch covers 1,2. So output indices: -1,0,1,2. Valid: 0,1,2. That's size 3. But formula says (2-1)*2 - 2*1 + 2 + 0 = 2. Wait, the formula in PyTorch docs for output size includes a +1: (H_in - 1) * stride - 2*padding + dilation*(kernel_size - 1) + output_padding + 1. With dilation=1: (2-1)*2 - 2 + (2-1) + 0 + 1 = 2 - 2 + 1 + 1 = 2. Wait that's 2. But my manual calc gave 3. Did I miscompute top-left? Maybe the formula for the top-left in scatter is h*stride - padding, but for K=2, the patch covers top-left to top-left+K-1 = h*stride - padding + K - 1. For h=1: 1*2 - 1 + 1 = 2. So covers 1,2. For h=0: -1,0. Valid outputs: 0,1,2. That's 3. But formula says 2. Something is inconsistent. Let's check a known case: ConvTranspose1d in PyTorch. Input size 1, stride=2, padding=0, kernel=3, outputpadding=0. H_out = (1-1)*2 - 0 + 3 + 0 = 3? Actually formula: (1-1)*2 - 0 + (3-1) + 0 + 1 = 3. Wait, with kernel_size=3: (1-1)*2 + (3-1) + 1 = 3. So output size 3. Input x0. Scatter: top-left = 0*2 - 0 = 0. Patch covers 0,1,2. Output size 3. Good. Now input size 2, stride=2, padding=1, kernel=2. H_out = (2-1)*2 - 2*1 + (2-1) + 0 + 1 = 2 - 2 + 1 + 1 = 2. So output size 2. Let's see. Input x0 at h=0: top-left = 0*2 - 1 = -1. Patch covers -1,0. Only 0 valid. Input x1 at h=1: top-left = 1*2 - 1 = 1. Patch covers 1,2. Only 1 valid (since size 2). So valid outputs: 0 and 1. Output size 2. That matches! My previous calculation incorrectly assumed output size 3 because I didn't clip to H_out. The scatter patches are clipped to the output bounds. So output size 2 means only indices 0 and 1 exist. x1's patch covers 1 and 2, but index 2 is out of bounds (since H_out=2, indices 0,1). So only index 1 is valid. So output size is indeed 2. Thus, the output bounds clip the scatter patches. Now with output_padding=1: H_out = (2-1)*2 - 2 + (2-1) + 1 + 1 = 2 - 2 + 1 + 1 + 1 = 3. Output size 3. Input x0: patch -1,0 => valid 0. Input x1: patch 1,2 => valid 1,2. So output indices 0,1,2. Great. So output_padding simply increases the output size, allowing more of the right/bottom edges of the scatter patches to be included. In terms of clipping, for our case H_out=256, the output bounds are [0,255]. The input is 128x128. For h=127: top-left = 127*2 - 1 = 253. Patch covers 253,254,255. All valid. For h=0: top-left = -1. Patch covers -1,0,1. Only 0,1 valid. So k_h=0 is clipped. For w=0: k_w=0 clipped. Output_padding=1 increases H_out from 255 to 256? Let's compute without output_padding: H_out = (128-1)*2 - 2*1 + 3 + 0 = 254 - 2 + 3 = 255. With output_padding=1: 256. So without output_padding, h=127 patch covers 253,254,255 (max index 255). That's exactly H_out=256? Wait, 255 is max index for size 255? No, size 255 means indices 0..254. Wait, 255 - 1 = 254. Let's recompute. If H_out=255, indices 0..254. Then h=127 patch covers 253,254,255. 255 is out of bounds. So k_h=2 would be clipped for h=127. With output_padding=1, H_out=256, indices 0..255, so 255 is valid. So output_padding allows the last kernel offset of the last input pixel to be included. Indeed, in our configuration: - h=0: top-left=-1, patch covers -1,0,1. Valid: 0,1. Missing k_h=0. - h=127: top-left=253, patch covers 253,254,255. Without output_padding, H_out=255, max index=254, so 255 invalid. Missing k_h=2. With output_padding=1, H_out=256, max index=255, all valid. So output_padding fixes the bottom/right clipping of the last input pixel. Similarly for w. Thus, the only clipped contributions are: - For h=0: k_h=0 is clipped (top edge). - For h=127: if output_padding were 0, k_h=2 would be clipped (bottom edge). But with output_padding=1, it's valid. So no bottom clipping. - For w=0: k_w=0 clipped (left edge). - For w=127: k_w=2 valid due to output_padding=1. What about interior pixels? For any h in [0,127], top-left = 2h-1. The patch covers 2h-1, 2h, 2h+1. For h=0: -1,0,1. For h=1: 1,2,3. All in [0,255]. For h=127: 253,254,255. All in [0,255]. So only h=0 has clipping on the top. Similarly only w=0 has clipping on the left. Wait, why doesn't h=0 have clipping on the bottom? It only covers up to 1, which is fine. h=127 covers up to 255, which is fine. So yes, only top and left edges are clipped. Great. Similarly, the first row of input (h=0) never uses k_h=0. The first column (w=0) never uses k_w=0. All other input pixels use all 3x3 kernel offsets. But is that true for all h? Let's check h=63: top-left = 125. Patch 125,126,127. All valid. So interior uses full kernel. Thus, the only boundary effect is the first row and first column. Wait, what about non-square? No, it's square. So the sum over output for each channel can be computed as: S[n, c_out] = sum_{c_in} sum_{h=0}^{127} sum_{w=0}^{127} x[n, c_in, h, w] * W_sum[c_in, c_out, h, w] where W_sum is: - For h>=1 and w>=1: W_sum = sum_{k_h=0..2, k_w=0..2} w_tr[c_in, c_out, k_h, k_w]. - For h=0, w>=1: W_sum = sum_{k_h=1..2, k_w=0..2} w_tr[c_in, c_out, k_h, k_w]. - For h>=1, w=0: W_sum = sum_{k_h=0..2, k_w=1..2} w_tr[c_in, c_out, k_h, k_w]. - For h=0, w=0: W_sum = sum_{k_h=1..2, k_w=1..2} w_tr[c_in, c_out, k_h, k_w]. But wait, we must also consider the bias. The sum of bias over output spatial is b[c_out] * (H_out * W_out) = b[c_out] * 256 * 256. So total sum of conv_transpose output = S[n,c_out] + b[c_out]*65536. Then final output = (S[n,c_out] + b[c_out]*65536) * multiplier / 65536 = S[n,c_out] * multiplier / 65536 + b[c_out] * multiplier. So we need to compute S[n,c_out] efficiently. We can compute S via a GEMM-like operation: reshape x to (N*H*W, C_in) and multiply by a matrix M of shape (C_in, C_out) where M_{c_in,c_out} = sum_{k_h,k_w} w_tr[c_in, c_out, k_h, k_w]. But we need to account for the first row and first column of x differently. Since only h=0 and w=0 are special, we can handle them separately. Specifically: x_interior = x[:, :, 1:, 1:] (size N, C_in, 127, 127) x_top = x[:, :, 0:1, 1:] (size N, C_in, 1, 127) x_left = x[:, :, 1:, 0:1] (size N, C_in, 127, 1) x_corner = x[:, :, 0:1, 0:1] (size N, C_in, 1, 1) We can sum x_interior over spatial dims: sum_interior = x_interior.sum(dim=[2,3]) shape (N, C_in). sum_top = x_top.sum(dim=[2,3]) shape (N, C_in). sum_left = x_left.sum(dim=[2,3]) shape (N, C_in). sum_corner = x_corner.squeeze? Actually sum_corner = x_corner.sum(dim=[2,3]) shape (N, C_in). Then: S = sum_interior * W_full + sum_top * W_top + sum_left * W_left + sum_corner * W_corner where: W_full[c_in, c_out] = sum_{k_h=0..2, k_w=0..2} w_tr[c_in, c_out, k_h, k_w]. W_top[c_in, c_out] = sum_{k_h=1..2, k_w=0..2} w_tr[c_in, c_out, k_h, k_w]. W_left[c_in, c_out] = sum_{k_h=0..2, k_w=1..2} w_tr[c_in, c_out, k_h, k_w]. W_corner[c_in, c_out] = sum_{k_h=1..2, k_w=1..2} w_tr[c_in, c_out, k_h, k_w]. Alternatively, we can combine these: W_full is the full sum. W_top = W_full - sum_{k_w} w_tr[:, :, 0, k_w]. W_left = W_full - sum_{k_h} w_tr[:, :, k_h, 0]. W_corner = W_full - sum_{k_w} w_tr[:, :, 0, k_w] - sum_{k_h} w_tr[:, :, k_h, 0] + w_tr[:, :, 0, 0]. Then S = sum_all * W_full - sum_top_row * sum_{k_w} w_tr[:, :, 0, k_w] - sum_left_col * sum_{k_h} w_tr[:, :, k_h, 0] + sum_corner * w_tr[:, :, 0, 0]. Wait, check: sum_all includes the top row and left column and corner. The interior is sum_all - sum_top_row - sum_left_col + sum_corner (since corner subtracted twice). Actually sum_interior = sum_all - sum_top_full - sum_left_full + sum_corner, where sum_top_full = x[:,:,0,:].sum() includes left corner. sum_left_full = x[:,:,:,0].sum() includes top corner. So interior = total - top - left + corner. Then S = interior * W_full + top * W_top + left * W_left + corner * W_corner. Simplify: S = (total - top - left + corner) * W_full + top * (W_full - row0_sum) + left * (W_full - col0_sum) + corner * (W_full - row0_sum - col0_sum + w000) = total * W_full - top * row0_sum - left * col0_sum + corner * w000. Where: row0_sum[c_in, c_out] = sum_{k_w=0..2} w_tr[c_in, c_out, 0, k_w]. col0_sum[c_in, c_out] = sum_{k_h=0..2} w_tr[c_in, c_out, k_h, 0]. w000[c_in, c_out] = w_tr[c_in, c_out, 0, 0]. Let's verify: W_top = W_full - row0_sum. W_left = W_full - col0_sum. W_corner = W_full - row0_sum - col0_sum + w000. Plug into S: S = interior*W_full + top*(W_full - row0) + left*(W_full - col0) + corner*(W_full - row0 - col0 + w000) = (interior+top+left+corner)*W_full - top*row0 - left*col0 - corner*row0 - corner*col0 + corner*w000. But interior+top+left+corner = total (sum over all spatial). So: S = total*W_full - (top+corner)*row0 - (left+corner)*col0 + corner*w000. Now top+corner = sum of first row (including corner). left+corner = sum of first column (including corner). Let's denote: sum_top_row = x[:, :, 0, :].sum(dim=[2])? Actually shape: x[:, :, 0, :] is (N, C_in, W). Sum over dim 2 => (N, C_in). sum_left_col = x[:, :, :, 0].sum(dim=[2]) => (N, C_in). sum_corner = x[:, :, 0, 0] => (N, C_in). Actually x[:,:,0,0] is (N, C_in). But sum_top_row already includes the corner. So top+corner = sum_top_row. left+corner = sum_left_col. Thus: S = total_sum * W_full - sum_top_row * row0_sum - sum_left_col * col0_sum + sum_corner * w000. Where total_sum = x.sum(dim=[2,3]) shape (N, C_in). This is a very nice formula! But wait, what about the fact that in conv_transpose2d, the output is generated by convolving the dilated input (padded with K-1-padding = 1 on each side and output_padding=1 on right/bottom). Yes, in the scatter view, each input pixel x[h,w] is multiplied by the kernel weight w[k_h,k_w] and added to output at (h*stride - padding + k_h, w*stride - padding + k_w). This is the standard definition of transposed convolution as a direct sum (col2im). The rows/columns that are clipped are those where output coordinate is out of bounds. But is this definition consistent with PyTorch's ConvTranspose2d? Yes, except for the weight layout: w_tr[c_in, c_out, k_h, k_w] is the weight for input channel c_in, output channel c_out. In the scatter view, x[n,c_in,h,w] is multiplied by w_tr[c_in,c_out,k_h,k_w] and added to y[n,c_out,h*stride - padding + k_h, w*stride - padding + k_w]. This should be correct for stride=1. For stride > 1, the "dilated" aspect is captured by the fact that the output coordinates are spaced out because of h*stride. For example, h=0 gives output at -1,0,1. h=1 gives 1,2,3. So output at 1 receives contributions from both h=0,k_h=2 and h=1,k_h=0. This matches the overlap we expect. Let's verify with a tiny script in PyTorch: random input, random weight. Now, we need to also consider whether the second mean over dimension [2,3] does anything. This is a series of GEMMs. We can do it in PyTorch without any CUDA kernel, and it would be extremely fast because it's just a few matrix multiplications with small C_in=64, C_out=128, N=16. Since the problem wants a custom CUDA kernel, we can write the reduction kernel in CUDA, and then do the rest in PyTorch. That should be very fast and correct. Let's write the reduction kernel. Each thread block handles one (n, c_in). There are N*C_in = 1024 blocks. Each block reduces over H*W = 16384 elements. With 256 threads per block, each thread processes 64 elements. The threads load x elements and accumulate into registers, then do a block-level reduction to get total, top, left, corner. But total requires sum over all H*W. top requires sum over w for h=0. left requires sum over h for w=0. corner is just x[n,c_in,0,0]. We can compute these in one pass over the elements. Pseudocode for block (n, c_in): total = 0 top = 0 left = 0 corner = x[n,c_in,0,0] (only one thread) for tid in range(threadIdx.x, H*W, blockDim.x): h = tid / W w = tid % W val = x[n,c_in,h,w] total += val if (h == 0) top += val if (w == 0) left += val // block reduce total, top, left Then store to total_buf[n,c_in], top_buf[n,c_in], left_buf[n,c_in], corner_buf[n,c_in]. Then in PyTorch: total = total_buf top = top_buf left = left_buf corner = corner_buf S = total.mm(W_full) - top.mm(row0) - left.mm(col0) + corner.mm(w000) out = S * multiplier / (H_out*W_out) + bias * multiplier Note: bias is a vector of length C_out. Then sum over k_h in [a,b], k_w in [c,d] = P[b,d] - P[a-1,d] - P[b,c-1] + P[a-1,c-1]. So we can precompute prefix sums of weights shape (C_in, C_out, K, K). The parameters are known at runtime. Kernel signature: ```cpp void reduce_for_convtranspose_sum( const float* x, int N, int C, int H, int W, float* out_total, float* out_top, float* out_left, float* out_corner ) ``` Grid: dim3(N, C, 1). Block: 256 threads. Inside kernel: ```cuda __global__ void reduce_kernel(const float* __restrict__ x, int N, int C, int H, int W, float* __restrict__ out_total, float* __restrict__ out_top, float* __restrict__ out_left, float* __restrict__ out_corner) { int n = blockIdx.x; int c = blockIdx.y; if (n >= N || c >= C) return; int spatial = H * W; const float* ptr = x + ((n * C + c) * H * W); float sum_total = 0.0f; float sum_top = 0.0f; float sum_left = 0.0f; float sum_corner = 0.0f; for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < spatial; idx += blockDim.x) { float val = ptr[idx]; sum_total += val; int h = idx / W; int w = idx - h * W; if (h == 0) sum_top += val; if (w == 0) sum_left += val; if (h == 0 && w == 0) sum_corner += val; } // block reduce __shared__ float s_total[256]; __shared__ float s_top[256]; __shared__ float s_left[256]; __shared__ float s_corner[256]; s_total[threadIdx.x] = sum_total; s_top[threadIdx.x] = sum_top; s_left[threadIdx.x] = sum_left; s_corner[threadIdx.x] = sum_corner; __syncthreads(); // parallel reduction for (int s = blockDim.x / 2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (threadIdx.x < s) { s_total[threadIdx.x] += s_total[threadIdx.x + s]; s_top[threadIdx.x] += s_top[threadIdx.x + s]; s_left[threadIdx.x] += s_left[threadIdx.x + s]; s_corner[threadIdx.x] += s_corner[threadIdx.x + s]; } __syncthreads(); } if (threadIdx.x == 0) { int out_idx = n * C + c; out_total[out_idx] = s_total[0]; out_top[out_idx] = s_top[0]; out_left[out_idx] = s_left[0]; out_corner[out_idx] = s_corner[0]; } } ``` This should be efficient. We can also add a second kernel that computes the final output, to avoid Python overhead. But Python overhead is negligible. Let's write the C++ wrapper: ```cpp torch::Tensor convtranspose_mean_forward( torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor w_full, torch::Tensor w_top, torch::Tensor w_left, torch::Tensor w_corner, torch::Tensor bias, float multiplier, int H_out, int W_out ) { int N = x.size(0); int C_in = x.size(1); int H = x.size(2); int W = x.size(3); int C_out = w_full.size(1); auto options = x.options(); auto sums = torch::empty({N, C_in, 4}, options); float* totals = sums.data_ptr<float>(); float* tops = totals + N*C_in; float* lefts = tops + N*C_in; float* corners = lefts + N*C_in; const float* x_ptr = x.data_ptr<float>(); reduce_kernel<<<dim3(N, C_in, 1), 256>>>(x_ptr, N, C_in, H, W, totals, tops, lefts, corners); // Then compute output via ATen auto total = sums.slice(2, 0, 1).squeeze(2); auto top_ = sums.slice(2, 1, 2).squeeze(2); auto left_ = sums.slice(2, 2, 3).squeeze(2); auto corner_ = sums.slice(2, 3, 4).squeeze(2); auto S = total.mm(w_full) - top_.mm(w_top) - left_.mm(w_left) + corner_.mm(w_corner); auto out = S * multiplier / float(H_out * W_out) + bias * multiplier; return out.unsqueeze(-1).unsqueeze(-1); } ``` Wait, `.slice` and `.squeeze` in C++ ATen are fine. `.mm` is matrix multiplication. Let's refine the C++ wrapper to compute everything on GPU: ```cpp torch::Tensor forward_cuda(torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor w_full, torch::Tensor w_top, torch::Tensor w_left, torch::Tensor w_corner, torch::Tensor bias, float multiplier, int H_out, int W_out) { int N = x.size(0); int C_in = x.size(1); int H = x.size(2); int W = x.size(3); int C_out = w_full.size(1); auto options = x.options(); // Allocate a tensor [N, C_in, 4] for the four sums auto sums = torch::empty({N, C_in, 4}, options); float* total_ptr = sums.data_ptr<float>(); float* top_ptr = total_ptr + N * C_in; float* left_ptr = top_ptr + N * C_in; float* corner_ptr = left_ptr + N * C_in; const float* x_ptr = x.data_ptr<float>(); dim3 grid(N, C_in); reduce_kernel<<<grid, 256>>>(x_ptr, N, C_in, H, W, total_ptr, top_ptr, left_ptr, corner_ptr); // Now compute output using ATen // Extract each sum auto total = sums.select(2, 0); // shape (N, C_in) auto top = sums.select(2, 1); auto left = sums.select(2, 2); auto corner = sums.select(2, 3); auto S = total.mm(w_full) - top.mm(w_top) - left.mm(w_left) + corner.mm(w_corner); auto out = S * multiplier / float(H_out * W_out) + bias * multiplier; return out.view({N, C_out, 1, 1}); } ``` Wait, does `sums.select(2,0)` return a 2D tensor? Yes, selecting index 0 on dim 2 of shape (N,C_in,4) gives (N,C_in). But we need to ensure it's contiguous? `.mm` requires the first tensor to be contiguous? Actually torch.mm in ATen handles strides. Let's do: `cpp_sources`: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> void reduce_kernel_launcher(const float* x, int N, int C, int H, int W, float* total, float* top, float* left, float* corner); torch::Tensor forward_cuda(torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor w_full, torch::Tensor w_top, torch::Tensor w_left, torch::Tensor w_corner, torch::Tensor bias, float multiplier) { // ... reduce_kernel_launcher(x_ptr, N, C_in, H, W, total_ptr, top_ptr, left_ptr, corner_ptr); // ... } ``` `cuda_sources`: ```cpp #include <torch/extension.h> __global__ void reduce_kernel(const float* __restrict__ x, int N, int C, int H, int W, float* __restrict__ total, float* __restrict__ top, float* __restrict__ left, float* __restrict__ corner) { // implementation } void reduce_kernel_launcher(const float* x, int N, int C, int H, int W, float* total, float* top, float* left, float* corner) { dim3 grid(N, C); reduce_kernel<<<grid, 256>>>(x, N, C, H, W, total, top, left, corner); } ``` Alternatively, we can skip the launcher and just declare `__global__ void reduce_kernel(...);` in `cpp_sources` and call it directly with `<<< >>>`. The C++ compiler in `load_inline` is actually nvcc for the whole thing? Let's check. `torch.utils.cpp_extension.load_inline` compiles `cpp_sources` with the C++ compiler and `cuda_sources` with nvcc. They are linked together. `cpp_sources` compiled with g++ won't understand `<<< >>>`. So we can't launch CUDA kernels in `cpp_sources`. We need a launcher function compiled with nvcc in `cuda_sources`. So the above approach is correct. Wait, actually `load_inline` documentation suggests that `cpp_sources` and `cuda_sources` are combined into a single extension module. The C++ code is compiled with the host compiler, CUDA with nvcc. To call a kernel from C++, you need a wrapper compiled with nvcc (in `cuda_sources`). So yes, we should define a launcher in `cuda_sources`. Let's structure the code: ```python import torch import torch.nn as nn from torch.utils.cpp_extension import load_inline cpp_source = """ #include <torch/extension.h> void reduce_kernel_launcher(const float* x, int N, int C, int H, int W, float* total, float* top, float* left, float* corner); torch::Tensor convtranspose_mean_forward( torch::Tensor x, torch::Tensor w_full, torch::Tensor w_top, torch::Tensor w_left, torch::Tensor w_corner, torch::Tensor bias, float multiplier, int H_out, int W_out ) { int N = x.size(0); int C_in = x.size(1); int H = x.size(2); int W = x.size(3); int C_out = w_full.size(1); auto options = x.options(); auto sums = torch::empty({N, C_in, 4}, options); float* total_ptr = sums.data_ptr<float>(); float* top_ptr = total_ptr + N * C_in; float* left_ptr = top_ptr + N * C_in; float* corner_ptr = left_ptr + N * C_in; reduce_kernel_launcher(x.data_ptr<float>(), N, C_in, H, W, total_ptr, top_ptr, left_ptr, corner_ptr); auto total = sums.select(2, 0); auto top = sums.select(2, 1); auto left = sums.select(2, 2); auto corner = sums.select(2, 3); auto S = total.mm(w_full) - top.mm(w_top) - left.mm(w_left) + corner.mm(w_corner); auto out = S * multiplier / float(H_out * W_out) + bias * multiplier; return out.view({N, C_out, 1, 1}); } """ cuda_source = """ #include <torch/extension.h> __global__ void reduce_kernel(const float* __restrict__ x, int N, int C, int H, int W, float* __restrict__ total, float* __restrict__ top, float* __restrict__ left, float* __restrict__ corner) { int n = blockIdx.x; int c = blockIdx.y; if (n >= N || c >= C) return; int spatial = H * W; const float* ptr = x + ((n * C + c) * spatial); float s_total = 0.0f; float s_top = 0.0f; float s_left = 0.0f; float s_corner = 0.0f; for (int idx = threadIdx.x; idx < spatial; idx += blockDim.x) { float val = ptr[idx]; s_total += val; int h = idx / W; int w = idx - h * W; if (h == 0) s_top += val; if (w == 0) s_left += val; if (h == 0 && w == 0) s_corner += val; } __shared__ float sh_total[256]; __shared__ float sh_top[256]; __shared__ float sh_left[256]; __shared__ float sh_corner[256]; sh_total[threadIdx.x] = s_total; sh_top[threadIdx.x] = s_top; sh_left[threadIdx.x] = s_left; sh_corner[threadIdx.x] = s_corner; __syncthreads(); for (int s = blockDim.x / 2; s > 0; s >>= 1) { if (threadIdx.x < s) { sh_total[threadIdx.x] += sh_total[threadIdx.x + s]; sh_top[threadIdx.x] += sh_top[threadIdx.x + s]; sh_left[threadIdx.x] += sh_left[threadIdx.x + s]; sh_corner[threadIdx.x] += sh_corner[threadIdx.x + s]; } __syncthreads(); } if (threadIdx.x == 0) { int out_idx = n * C + c; total[out_idx] = sh_total[0]; top[out_idx] = sh_top[0]; left[out_idx] = sh_left[0]; corner[out_idx] = sh_corner[0]; } } void reduce_kernel_launcher(const float* x, int N, int C, int H, int W, float* total, float* top, float* left, float* corner) { dim3 grid(N, C); reduce_kernel<<<grid, 256>>>(x, N, C, H, W, total, top, left, corner); } """ convtranspose_mean = load_inline( name='convtranspose_mean', cpp_sources=cpp_source, cuda_sources=cuda_source, functions=['convtranspose_mean_forward'], extra_cuda_cflags=['-O3'], verbose=False ) ``` Then `ModelNew`: ```python class ModelNew(nn.Module): def __init__(self, in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride, padding, output_padding, multiplier): super(ModelNew, self).__init__() self.conv_transpose = nn.ConvTranspose2d(in_channels, out_channels, kernel_size, stride=stride, padding=padding, output_padding=output_padding) self.multiplier = multiplier w = self.conv_transpose.weight # (in, out, K, K) K = kernel_size p = padding # Precompute aggregates w_full = w.sum(dim=[2, 3]).contiguous() if p > 0: w_top = w[:, :, p:, :].sum(dim=[2, 3]).contiguous() w_left = w[:, :, :, p:].sum(dim=[2, 3]).contiguous() w_corner = w[:, :, p:, p:].sum(dim=[2, 3]).contiguous() else: w_top = w_full.clone() w_left = w_full.clone() w_corner = w_full.clone() self.register_buffer('w_full', w_full) self.register_buffer('w_top', w_top) self.register_buffer('w_left', w_left) self.register_buffer('w_corner', w_corner) def forward(self, x): N, C_in, H, W = x.shape H_out = (H - 1) * self.conv_transpose.stride[0] - 2 * self.conv_transpose.padding[0] + self.conv_transpose.kernel_size[0] + self.conv_transpose.output_padding[0] W_out = (W - 1) * self.conv_transpose.stride[1] - 2 * self.conv_transpose.padding[1] + self.conv_transpose.kernel_size[1] + self.conv_transpose.output_padding[1] bias = self.conv_transpose.bias if self.conv_transpose.bias is not None else torch.zeros(self.conv_transpose.out_channels, device=x.device, dtype=x.dtype) # Ensure all are contiguous and on same device out = convtranspose_mean.convtranspose_mean_forward( x.contiguous(), self.w_full, self.w_top, self.w_left, self.w_corner, bias, float(self.multiplier), int(H_out), int(W_out) ) return out ``` Wait, `self.conv_transpose.stride[0]` etc. in forward? Actually `self.conv_transpose.stride` is a tuple. We can just use the constructor args or read from the module. Reading from module is fine. One nuance: `bias` shape should be (C_out,) and we need to broadcast it in the C++ code. In the C++ code, we do `bias * multiplier`. If bias is a 1D tensor of shape (C_out,), and `S` is (N, C_out), then `bias * multiplier` is automatically broadcasted. Wait, C++ ATen overloaded operators handle broadcasting: `out = S * (multiplier / (H_out*W_out)) + bias * multiplier;`. Here `bias` is (C_out). `S` is (N, C_out). Now, let's check the correctness of the reduction. I realize that `sums.select(2,0)` returns a view with non-unit stride in the last dimension. For `mm`, we need the inner dimension to be contiguous?
